He produced operas in each of the prevailing styles: <a href="/wiki/Opera_buffa" title="Opera buffa">opera buffa</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Marriage_of_Figaro" title="The Marriage of Figaro">The Marriage of Figaro</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Giovanni" title="Don Giovanni">Don Giovanni</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Cos%C3%AC_fan_tutte" title="Così fan tutte">Così fan tutte</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Opera_seria" title="Opera seria">opera seria</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Idomeneo" title="Idomeneo">Idomeneo</a></i>; and <a href="/wiki/Singspiel" title="Singspiel">Singspiel</a>, of which <i><a href="/wiki/The_Magic_Flute" title="The Magic Flute">Die Zauberflöte</a></i> is the most famous example by any composer. In his later operas, he employed subtle changes in instrumentation, orchestral texture, and <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">tone colour</a>, for emotional depth and to mark dramatic shifts. Here his advances in opera and instrumental composing interacted: his increasingly sophisticated use of the orchestra in the symphonies and concertos influenced his operatic orchestration, and his developing subtlety in using the orchestra to psychological effect in his operas was in turn reflected in his later non-operatic compositions.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h3>
<p>Mozart's most famous pupil, whom the Mozarts took into their Vienna home for two years as a child, was probably <a href="/wiki/Johann_Nepomuk_Hummel" title="Johann Nepomuk Hummel">Johann Nepomuk Hummel</a>, a transitional figure between Classical and Romantic eras.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> More important is the influence Mozart had on composers of later generations. Ever since the surge in his reputation after his death, studying his scores has been a standard part of classical musicians' training.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a>, Mozart's junior by fifteen years, was deeply influenced by his work, with which he was acquainted as a teenager.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> He is thought to have performed Mozart's operas while playing in the court orchestra at <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> and travelled to Vienna in 1787 hoping to study with the older composer. Some of <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Ludwig_van_Beethoven#Instrumental_works:_WoO_1–86" title="List of compositions by Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven's works</a> have direct models in comparable works by Mozart, and he wrote <a href="/wiki/Cadenza" title="Cadenza">cadenzas</a> (<a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a>&#160;58) to Mozart's D minor piano concerto <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._20_(Mozart)" title="Piano Concerto No. 20 (Mozart)">K.&#160;466</a>. For further details, see <a href="/wiki/Mozart_and_Beethoven" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozart and Beethoven">Mozart and Beethoven</a>.
</p><p>Composers have paid homage to Mozart by writing sets of <a href="/wiki/Variation_(music)" title="Variation (music)">variations</a> on his themes. Beethoven wrote four such sets (Op.&#160;66, WoO&#160;28, WoO&#160;40, WoO&#160;46). Others include <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Sor" title="Fernando Sor">Fernando Sor</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Introduction_and_Variations_on_a_Theme_by_Mozart_(Sor)" title="Introduction and Variations on a Theme by Mozart (Sor)">Introduction and Variations on a Theme by Mozart</a> (1821), <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Glinka" title="Mikhail Glinka">Mikhail Glinka</a>'s Variations on a Theme from Mozart's Opera "<a href="/wiki/The_Magic_Flute" title="The Magic Flute">Die Zauberflöte</a>" (1822), <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin" title="Frédéric Chopin">Frédéric Chopin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Variations_on_%22L%C3%A0_ci_darem_la_mano%22_(Chopin)" title="Variations on &quot;Là ci darem la mano&quot; (Chopin)">Variations on "Là ci darem la mano"</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Giovanni" title="Don Giovanni">Don Giovanni</a></i> (1827), and <a href="/wiki/Max_Reger" title="Max Reger">Max Reger</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Variations_and_Fugue_on_a_Theme_by_Mozart" title="Variations and Fugue on a Theme by Mozart">Variations and Fugue on a Theme by Mozart</a> (1914), based on the variation theme in the piano sonata <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._11_(Mozart)" title="Piano Sonata No. 11 (Mozart)">K.&#160;331</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Ilyich_Tchaikovsky" title="Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky">Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky</a> wrote his Orchestral Suite No.&#160;4 in G, <i><a href="/wiki/Orchestral_Suite_No._4_Mozartiana_(Tchaikovsky)" title="Orchestral Suite No. 4 Mozartiana (Tchaikovsky)">Mozartiana</a></i> (1887), as a tribute to Mozart.
</p>
<h3><span id="K.C3.B6chel_catalogue"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Köchel_catalogue">Köchel catalogue</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6chel_catalogue" title="Köchel catalogue">Köchel catalogue</a></div>
<p>For unambiguous identification of works by Mozart, a <i>Köchel catalogue number</i> is used. This is a unique number assigned, in regular chronological order, to every one of his known works. A work is referenced by the abbreviation "K." or "KV" followed by this number. The first edition of the catalogue was completed in 1862 by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Ritter_von_K%C3%B6chel" title="Ludwig Ritter von Köchel">Ludwig von Köchel</a>. It has since been repeatedly updated, as scholarly research improves knowledge of the dates and authenticity of individual works.
</p>
